#NOW 2007.07.14 23:54:00

It probably pays to think of my life as a set of backlogs.  The goal
is to not allow any backlog to add up, while my most important
project is the one in which I'm working to actively push back the
backlog.  Many things which seem difficult to quantify feel much more
doable if I think of them in terms of stories in a journal.  For
example, "Love Isaac" is very abstract, but if I can't think of a
unique story about Isaac to write in my journal for the last few days,
my relationship with Isaac is probably in trouble.

So my weight is a perfect example.  Unless it's currently my top
project, I don't necessarily need to lose weight.  (eureka)  Instead,
I need to maintain my current weight.  But goals in a maintenance
phase must be actionable, and I can't immediately lose weight, short
of hacking off limbs.  But I can maintain a different metric, for
example "(Pounds over 172) - (Fasting hours)".  If I gain weight, I
can immediately bump up my fasting hours to compensate.

Another difficult point are things that require dead time.  For
example, I can't keep the number of minutes since I've logged about
Isaac at 0.  One possibility is keeping "number of days (if more than
one) since I've logged about Isaac."  That's a little better, but nor
super.  Now, I guess it's up to me to choose some magic time interval
after which logging about Isaac immediately becomes necessary.
Perhaps better would be to somehow capture the average number of log
entries per (week, month, year).  That way, if I'm doing better this
year than last, I'm still OK, and I could create a project to up that
total.  Again, I would need to choose a perfect interval.  Or, if it's
been 2X days since I started the goal, the measure could be (number of
times I did it in the first X days) - (number of times I've done it in
the last X days).

Of course, part of the goal will be to recognize constraints.
Sleeping is a big concern in a system like this.

And again, how do I manage projects with deadlines?  I recognize them,
make goals out of time worked, and move forward from there, I guess.

Most awesome would be a system that worked directly from a journal
like this one: a certain entered symbol would create a goal, another
would indicate the current condition of the goal.  Create a project,
make progress toward a project.  Create a task, complete a task,
rename a task.  Actually, this is awesome, right?  It's a perfect way
to start with something meaningful, keep a full audit of what I'm
doing, and still be able to automate as I move forward.  What do I
need again?

- Have an idea
- Officially start tracking a goal
- Current condition of the goal
- Start working on a project
- Identify next step for a project (which is a task)
- Commit to an independent task
- Do a task
- Finish a project
- Edit a task

That will work for now.  OK, obviously I like to use "-" as a prefix
in my normal writing, so that's out.  Actually, I like the
preprocessor idea from C.  The command character is #.  Only if it is
at the beginning of a line, or the first character within delimiters,
will it count.  So, if \# were #, the following would be good:

\#IDEA: Do something cool

That would be cool (\#IDEA: do it!)

Very nice.  In that case, the command sequences I'm thinking of are:

- #NOW timestamp
- #IDEA plain text
- #START_GOAL id (description should follow)
- #TRACK id: number
- #START_PROJECT id (description should follow)
- #PROGRESS id of goal or project, or task description
- #NEXT_STEP project_id: task description
- #NEW_TASK task description
- #DONE task description or project_id
- #EDIT old description -> new description

Goals and projects have ids because I expect to be typing them a lot.
Tasks don't: they should only come up if edited or done, and I want to
prevent myself from getting too excited about them.  If more
information needs to be added about anything, I should do it under
"PROGRESS" for that item: it's progress to be able to know more than I
did before.

#IDEA: pull in ideas from ideas.txt

Separating ideas from tasks is good.  Things I might do vs. things I
must do.  Goals and projects are a little dodgier, since the
nomenclature is a little odd on goals.  Most of the goals are things I
want to continue doing throughout my life, which I might be "behind"
on in some sense, whereas projects are pushes that I expect to have
limited duration.

and tasks should be nameable on one line.  The -- underneath to
indicate a quick side though on the line above seems doable--this is
not something that an automated tool should necessarily recognize.

#START_GOAL: Dishes

hour), or GOOD (alles gut).

#NOW 2007-07-30 21:01:40

I'm getting really stuck on the OOPSLA Theory demo camera-ready copy.  

Things I might want to expand on: I think the idea in the abstract
about "worrying" is a great point to expand on: if developers were
great at looking at and thinking about their code and deciding whether
it had the desired behavior, we'd never have to write tests in the
first place.  Writing tests only for a few scenarios assumes that
developers are great at looking at their code and determining that
there are no unexpected paths, which is the same skill we're assuming
they don't have.

I don't talk about design by contract at all.

Do I give Tillman and Schulte enough credit?

Auto-creation of stubs

Situations in which theories work well:
- Identities
- Data conversion
- General allowed behaviors
- Inconsequential data
- Intention
- Stub-style collaboration

Tests are good for partial specs, theories for more complete specs.

Watching out for duplication in Theories.

Impact on design beyond TDD

#NOW 2007-08-01 12:19:14

I'm talking to Marat in 40 minutes.  First on the table is getting the
case study restarted.  Last week, the problem was that inspecting the
code, which had to be done in the manual JUnit case, seemed to swamp
the other techniques in the number of issues found.  In the end, what
I want is a test suite that is closer to the ideal test suite--it
finds bugs that I had not considered before, but that now seem
likely.  How do I determine which bugs are likely?  I look at the
code.  This seems reasonable for developers--either they have just
written the code, or understanding it appears to be a high priority.
The alternative is preventing anyone from looking at the code, but
this could be a problem if the test suite is already weak.

So, then, the team members get a certain amount of time just to scan
through the code and note doubts (if possible, we'd like those
notes).  Then they can begin using dynamic feedback--any of the four
techniques earlier discussed.

This should work.  I should try it out myself first, but I don't have
time just yet.  Hopefully soon.

The second thing is that either Marat, or someone he works with,
should write a Theory.

#NOW 2007-08-09 14:41:01

So manually messing with the backlog is getting annoying.  I'm tempted
by the idea that moving things into milestones is a lot like
"bucketizing" ideas in glinda.  Differences:

- Backlog entries may have different time estimates
- Backlog milestones have timeboxes
- It might be nice to have things automatically slide from one timebox
  to the next.  On the other hand, having to explicitly move things
  down might aid thinking about things

Why am I so hung up?  Let's just do this.  On the other hand, I
haven't prayed in weeks.

#NOW 2007-08-09 16:39:02
#resume FirstAgitarBackLog

Here's the thought: there's two things going on in a backlog.  First,
each of my ideas might be to the benefit of one or more parties.
Second is the idea of a milestone chain.  Each node in the chain has a
name, and each node knows who the nodes before and after are.  There's
also time commitments between each node.  There's also time estimates
on ideas.  Interestingly, this could also work for a money budgeting
system, but let's not get ahead of ourselves.  That's more than two
things going on.  There's also the fact that these ideas still belong
to the standard buckets: if something important needs to be done for
Agitar today, I still want it in the "day" bucket.

#NOW 2007-08-23 07:24:44

Praying.

Lord,

This morning, I feel ashamed.  I am fat.  I could have done much more
to help with family camp.  I could have done much more to be ready to
meet with Marat on Monday.  I have failed to get Becca the kitchen she
wanted.  I continually fail to take appropriate responsibility as
husband and father in this family.  I constantly cry out for Becca's
approval, when I should seek yours.  Under much more stressful
conditions, endured for much more righteous reasons, Paul kept the
faith--I have abandoned it so many times.  As recently as yesterday I
eagerly sought out my old sexual temptations, a foreign God to whom I
so often turn to help instead of you.

I am sorry.  I want to, right now, start a new path.  Today, Paul says
"judge nothing before the appointed time".  The time to determine
whether or not I am a failure has not yet come.  The jury is still
out.  My choices in the next instant are as important as yesterday's,
or last year's.

I would love to end today feeling that I had been responsible with
what I ate.  I would like to be positive, secure in your blessings,
and use that security to encourage others.

I'd like Becca to have a chance to read her Bible, as well.  She's
been so tired lately.

I thought that we were supposed to bring coffee for the early-morning
coffee time.  Becca believes there's still coffee down at the mess
hall.  Originally, I gave in because she's Becca, and I always give in
to her.  But she's right--Kris didn't say that there specifically
wouldn't be mess hall coffee, and it would be more confusing to people
if there were coffee in both places.

Thank you for every way that you help me,

   David

import com.agitar.lib.junit.AgitarTestCase;
import java.util.List;
import org.junit.Assert;

import org.mortbay.io.BufferCache;
import org.mortbay.io.ByteArrayBuffer;
import org.mortbay.io.View;
import org.mortbay.io.nio.NIOBuffer;

public class BufferTheoriesAgitarTest extends AgitarTestCase {
    
    public Class getTargetClass()  {
        return BufferTheories.class;
    }

        new BufferTheories();
        assertTrue("Test call resulted in expected outcome", true);
    }
    
    public void testBufferExercise() throws Throwable {
        Buffer byteArrayBuffer = new ByteArrayBuffer(100);
        new BufferTheories().bufferExercise(byteArrayBuffer);
        assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) byteArrayBuffer.array()[0]", (byte)2, ((ByteArrayBuffer) byteArrayBuffer).array()[0]);
    }
    
    public void testBufferHash() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer(100);
        new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b1, "testString", "teststring");
        assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.array()[0]", (byte)116, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).array()[0]);
    }

        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferExercise(new View(new NIOBuffer(100, true)));
            fail("Expected AssertionError to be thrown");
        } catch (AssertionError ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "message expected:<99> but was:<97>", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assert.class, ex);
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferExerciseThrowsAssertionError1() throws Throwable {
        Buffer caseInsensitive = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive(new ByteArrayBuffer(100).array(), 100, 0, 1000);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferExercise(caseInsensitive);
            fail("Expected AssertionError to be thrown");
        } catch (AssertionError ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "message expected:<100> but was:<0>", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assert.class, ex);
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferExerciseThrowsAssumptionViolatedException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer caseInsensitive = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive(new ByteArrayBuffer(100).array(), 100, -1, 1000);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferExercise(caseInsensitive);
            fail("Expected AssumptionViolatedException to be thrown");

            new BufferTheories().bufferExercise(null);

    public void testBufferHashThrowsArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive(new ByteArrayBuffer(100).array(), 100, 1000, 0);
        Buffer b2 = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive("testBufferTheoriess");
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "testString", "teststring");
            fail("Expected ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException to be thrown");
        } catch (ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "1099", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(ByteArrayBuffer.class, ex);
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsAssertionError() throws Throwable {

            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "testString", "teststring");
            fail("Expected AssertionError to be thrown");
        } catch (AssertionError ex) {
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.array()[0]", (byte)116, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).array()[0]);
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "expected:<305994429> but was:<-1>", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assert.class, ex);
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.capacity()", 100, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).capacity());
            assertEquals("(NIOBuffer) b2.capacity()", 0, ((NIOBuffer) b2).capacity());
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsAssumptionViolatedException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer(new ByteArrayBuffer(100).array());
        Buffer b2 = new BufferCache.CachedBuffer("testBufferTheoriesValue", 100);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "", "");
            fail("Expected AssumptionViolatedException to be thrown");
        } catch (Assume.AssumptionViolatedException ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "got: <false>, expected: is <true>", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assume.class, ex);
            assertEquals("ex.fValue", Boolean.FALSE, getPrivateField(ex, "fValue"));
            assertNotNull("ex.fMatcher", getPrivateField(ex, "fMatcher"));
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.capacity()", 100, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).capacity());
            assertEquals("(BufferCache.CachedBuffer) b2.getOrdinal()", 100, ((BufferCache.CachedBuffer) b2).getOrdinal());
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsAssumptionViolatedException1() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new BufferCache.CachedBuffer("testBufferTheoriesValue", 100);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b1, "testBufferTheoriesS1", "testBufferTheoriesS2");
            fail("Expected AssumptionViolatedException to be thrown");
        } catch (Assume.AssumptionViolatedException ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "got: <false>, expected: is <true>", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assume.class, ex);
            assertEquals("ex.fValue", Boolean.FALSE, getPrivateField(ex, "fValue"));
            assertNotNull("ex.fMatcher", getPrivateField(ex, "fMatcher"));
            assertEquals("(BufferCache.CachedBuffer) b1.getOrdinal()", 100, ((BufferCache.CachedBuffer) b1).getOrdinal());
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsAssumptionViolatedException2() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b2 = new ByteArrayBuffer(100);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(null, b2, "testBufferTheoriesS1", "testBufferTheoriesS2");
            fail("Expected AssumptionViolatedException to be thrown");
        } catch (Assume.AssumptionViolatedException ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "got: <[null, , testBufferTheoriesS1, testBufferTheoriesS2]>, expected: each not null", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(Assume.class, ex);
            assertEquals("ex.fValue.size()", 4, ((List) getPrivateField(ex, "fValue")).size());
            assertNotNull("ex.fMatcher", getPrivateField(ex, "fMatcher"));
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b2.capacity()", 100, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b2).capacity());
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsIllegalArgumentException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer(100);
        Buffer b2 = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive("1".getBytes(), 100, 0, 1000);
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "testString", "teststring");
            fail("Expected IllegalArgumentException to be thrown");
        } catch (IllegalArgumentException ex) {
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.array()[0]", (byte)116, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).array()[0]);
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "index>capacity(): 100>1", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(AbstractBuffer.class, ex);
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.capacity()", 100, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).capacity());
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsIllegalStateException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive("testString");
        Buffer b2 = new ByteArrayBuffer.CaseInsensitive("testString");
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "testString", "teststring");
            fail("Expected IllegalStateException to be thrown");
        } catch (IllegalStateException ex) {
            assertEquals("ex.getMessage()", "READONLY", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(AbstractBuffer.class, ex);
        }
    }
    
    public void testBufferHashThrowsNullPointerException() throws Throwable {
        Buffer b1 = new ByteArrayBuffer(new ByteArrayBuffer(100).array());
        Buffer b2 = new View();
        try {
            new BufferTheories().bufferHash(b1, b2, "testString", "teststring");
            fail("Expected NullPointerException to be thrown");
        } catch (NullPointerException ex) {
            assertNull("ex.getMessage()", ex.getMessage());
            assertThrownBy(View.class, ex);
            assertEquals("(ByteArrayBuffer) b1.capacity()", 100, ((ByteArrayBuffer) b1).capacity());
        }
    }
}

Using the Bible Gateway verse of the day as a starting point, I'll
start today reading in Isaiah 46.

"The images that are carried about are burdensome, a burden to the
weary".  I certainly feel burdened right now, Lord.  It's going on
three hours since I talked to Mike, and I still don't have a single
word checked in.  I told him that I have done case studies that I
really haven't done.  Why do I keep trying to cover for myself?  Why
not just be honest?

Marat's around.  He'll be wanting to talk in about 20 minutes.  That
will be more time gone.  You said that your yoke was easy, and your
burden was light.  Does this mean it's time to just let go of the
paper?  Or to just let go of trying to seem more done than I am?

Where do my burdens come from?  First, I really want to submit to
ICSE, and I'm worried I'll miss it.  Second, I really want Marat and
Mike, and he reviewers, to think I'm cool, and right now, I feel like
an idiot.  Third, I'm worried that there is a way that I could get
everything done, but the kids and Becca and Mom will be hurt by
it--even worse, there's a way that I might not get enough done on
time, but the kids would still get hurt.  Fourth, I've basically lied
to Mike, and I would feel both stupid and guilty if he found out.
Fifth, deep down I'm pretty sure that I could be working more
efficiently--for example, I could have not answered my mail, or not be
messing with my glinda list, or not praying right now, but I am.

First, if it would destroy your plan for my life for me not to submit
to ICSE, then the submission will be made.  Really what I'm asking, if
I ask you to guarantee we get a good submission made, is protection
from pain, especially pain that results in large part from my own
actions.  I don't want to feel guilty anymore.  Let's face it.  If I
could have come up with a way not to need Glinda just to get my work
done, I'd be much further along now.  I think that experimenting with
Glinda was the right choice.  However, if I really believe that, why
am I hiding its existence from Marat and Mike?  Because I know that it
was not the right way to go--it was big toolmaking up front, instead
of little toolmaking along the way.  If I would feel ashamed publicly
asking for help on a project, I should not be doing it.

I don't know if I feel better.  I feel that I need your forgiveness,
but I'm not sure if what I really need is forgiveness from Marat and
Mike.  Help me.

   David

package test.net.saff.ethan.mountaingame;

import static org.hamcrest.CoreMatchers.is;
import static org.hamcrest.CoreMatchers.not;
import static org.junit.Assert.assertFalse;
import static org.junit.Assert.assertThat;
import static org.junit.Assert.assertTrue;
import static org.junit.matchers.JUnitMatchers.containsString;

import org.junit.Assume;
import org.junit.Before;
import org.junit.Test;
import org.junit.experimental.theories.DataPoint;
import org.junit.experimental.theories.DataPoints;
import org.junit.experimental.theories.Theories;
import org.junit.experimental.theories.Theory;
import org.junit.runner.RunWith;
import org.junit.runners.Enclosed;

@RunWith(Enclosed.class)
public class TestEthanHasFun {
	@RunWith(Theories.class)
	public static class AtAnyTime {
		@DataPoints
		public static Player[] players = { new Player() };

		@DataPoint
		public static Player winningPlayer() {
			Player ethan = new Player();
			ethan.answerRight();
			ethan.answerRight();
			ethan.answerRight();
			ethan.answerRight();
			ethan.answerRight();
			ethan.answerRight();
			return ethan;
		}

		@Theory
		public void winTheGame(Player ethan) {
			Assume.assumeThat(ethan.points(), is(6));
			assertTrue(ethan.wins());
		}
	}

	@RunWith(Theories.class)
	public static class AtBeginning extends Player {
		// TODO (Sep 20, 2007 6:17:41 PM): Why no tests for AtBeginning from JUnit Factory?

		@DataPoints public static String[] strings = {"Ethan", "Isaac"};
		@Before
		public void restartGame() {
			startGame();
		}

		@Test
		public void matchEverest() {
			showPictureOfEverest();
			showNamesOfMountainRanges("Himalayas", "Andes", "Rockies", "Alps",
					"European Highlands");
			dragEverestToMountainRangeCalled("Himalayas");
			assertThat(points(), is(1));
		}

		@Test
		public void loseTheGame() {
			answerSixWrong();
			assertTrue(loses());
		}

		@Test
		public void failToWinTheGame() {
			answerSixWrong();
			assertFalse(wins());
		}

		@Test
		public void getOneLosePoint() {
			dragEverestToMountainRangeCalled("Rockies");
			assertThat(loosePoints(), is(1));
		}

		@Test
		public void avoidGettingALosePoint() {
			answerRight();
			assertThat(loosePoints(), is(0));
		}

		@Test
		public void startWithZeroPoints() {
			assertThat(points(), is(0));
		}

		@Test
		public void playAgain() {
			answerSixRight();
			assertThat(shownText(), containsString("You get to play again"));
		}

		@Test
		public void avoidLosing() {
			answerSixRight();
			assertFalse(loses());
		}

		@Test
		public void avoidSeeingReplayMessage() {
			answerSixWrong();
			assertThat(shownText(),
					not(containsString("You get to play again")));
		}
		
		@Theory public void findArbitraryShownText(String toShow) {
			showText(toShow);
			assertThat(shownText(), containsString(toShow));
		}

		private void answerSixRight() {
			answerRight();
			answerRight();
			answerRight();
			answerRight();
			answerRight();
			answerRight();
		}

		private void answerSixWrong() {
			answerWrong();
			answerWrong();
			answerWrong();
			answerWrong();
			answerWrong();
			answerWrong();
		}

		private void dragEverestToMountainRangeCalled(String range) {
			// TODO Auto-generated method stub
			if (range.equals("Himalayas"))
				answerRight();
			else
				answerWrong();
		}

		private void showNamesOfMountainRanges(String... ranges) {
			// TODO Auto-generated method stub

		}

		private void showPictureOfEverest() {
			// TODO Auto-generated method stub

		}
	}
}

#NOW 2007-10-09 21:04:09

Commit message:

- Simplified Theory code by assuming that all datapoints are static
  (every one I've ever written is)

- Removed EachTestNotifier from Assignments (makes the class more
  cohesive, but requires the notifier to get passed around a bunch.
  TODO added to look at this.)

- Extracted AllMembersSupplier, absorbed AssignmentRequest into
  Assignments.  Better cohesion, less coupling

- EachTestNotifier.withNewInstance is no longer needed.  It was a
  nasty little beast and will not be missed.

- Added comment to ErrorList TODO

- Added TestMethod.getTestClass--will probably change in upcoming
  model package revamp anyway

#NOW 2007-10-10 11:32:53

Made Link unable to throw exception.  The score:

LOSERS:

- Theories got more complicated, but it's possible this pushes it
  further toward being Link-based, which I'd like

- ExpectedException: slightly more complicated

- IgnoreTest / Notifying: now need to take in EachTestNotifier, but
  the advantage is that it's now explict that they're the only ones
  that use the full power of that.

- Invoke: absorbs complexity from elsewhere

was

	public void run(EachTestNotifier context) throws Throwable {
		fTestMethod.invokeExplosively(fTarget);
	}

is

	public void run(FailureListener listener) {
		try {
			fTestMethod.invokeExplosively(fTarget);
		} catch (Throwable e) {
			listener.addFailure(e);
		}
	}

WINNERS:

- JUnit4ClassRunner

was

    try {
    	chain(method, test).run(context);
    } catch (StoppedByUserException e) {
    	throw e;
    } catch (Throwable e) {
    	throw new RuntimeException("Unexpected error running tests", e);
    }

is

    chain(method, test, context).run(context)

- Notifying

was

	context.fireTestStarted();
	try {
		fNext.run(context);
	} catch (Throwable e) {
		context.addFailure(e);
	} finally {
		context.fireTestFinished();
	}

is

	fNotifier.fireTestStarted();
	fNext.run(listener);
	fNotifier.fireTestFinished();

- WithBeforeAndAfter can now use JavaElement, eliminating earlier
  duplication

- WithTimeout

was

	try {
		fNext.run(context);
	} catch (Exception e) {
		throw e;
	} catch (Error e) {
		throw e;
	} catch (Throwable e) {
		// TODO: (Oct 5, 2007 11:27:11 AM) Now what?  Is there a useful thing to do with this?
	}

is

	fNext.run(listener);

#NOW 2007-10-12 20:02:14

OK, so I've screwed up velocity.  The idea is supposed to be that if I
usually do something every three days, and it's been three days since
I've done it, I should do it again.  Perhaps that's the answer.
However, for things like sleeping, that I usually do every day for
multiple "points", I don't want it figuring that I usually sleep once
every eight hours or so.  If I've done something in the last 24 hours,
then the current system works.  If not, I need to figure it based on
the last positive track.  So it's 24 hours ago, if there's been a
positive track since, or 24 hours since the last positive track, that
is the comparison.  So one test would be:

(t0)
t1: 1
t2: 2
t3: [imaginary end point]
t4: 
t5: now.

We compare the velocity at t3 (3/3 = 1.0).  We should be 2 behind now,
sice we were pulling one a day.  Let's try it.

#NOW 2007-10-21 14:04:47

Today at lunch, Ethan just couldn't get enough green pasta.  So Isaac
wandered off to play with trains, and Ethan and I talked.  Without
being really sure what to talk about, I pulled out stories about when
I was Ethan's age.  I talked about preschool at Grace Church United,
told him I grew up in St. Peters, not St. Louis proper.  I told him
about the blocks we had in preschool, and the animals--how we used to
build zoos out of blocks.  I told him that I loved reading books about
animals, and acting out my favorite scenes from the Empire Strikes
Back.  He said that when he's more grown up, he'd like to see that
movie with me.  He told me the same about the Voyage of the Dawn
Treader.  Ethan really is interested in who I am, when I give him the
chance to be.

#NOW 2007-10-21 14:07:28

#idea Reduce libraries needed by Theory Explorer
#NOW 2007-10-21 16:11:59
 
How to get stuff from Agitar VPN through tunneling:

To see wiki:

sudo ssh -L 80:wiki.in.agitar.com:80 -l david -N knight.agitar.com
Agitar password is d@v1d
Surf to, for example, http://localhost/twiki/bin/view/Agitar/SubversionForDevelopers
Due to hard-coded URL's, many links have to be hand-edited

To get svn:

ssh -L 1234:raptor:80 -l david -N knight.agitar.com
Now, you can surf to http://localhost:1234/repos/
And check things out: svn co http://localhost:1234/repos/build.system/trunk build.system

Now, to get everything:

SVNURL=http://localhost:1234/repos build.system/ant -f build.system/build.xml svn-all-modules

#startProject WritePurposeOfUserStudy

The adoption rate of theories does not appear to be high.  Quick questions to answer:

- How many downloads of junit 4.4?
- How many of those are legit?
- How many mailing list postings about theories?
- How many about other 4.4-specific features?
- Of JUnit tests, how many are theorizable?

If adoption is truly low, why is this?

- New features take a long time to disseminate?
- Theories are poorly documented?
- Developers don't think in theories?
- Big ideas aren't theorizable?
- Current code isn't theorizable

then backup, and then restore.

#done LookupHowToDoIt

#NOW 2007-11-07 11:44:08
#track InboxSize 0
#wait Lunchtime 17 minutes
#nextStep TransferComvatars HowToBackupAndRestoreMySql

#NOW 2007-11-07 11:51:59

It looks like you back up with

mysqldump --all-databases > all_databases.sql

and restore with

mysql -p --user=username < guestdb.txt

I've bookmarked both pages where I found these.

Changes in this check-in:
- Moved InitializationError to ParentRunner, since it was only used by
  subclasses of ParentRunner.
- Broke up TestMethod into FrameworkMethod (which makes it more clear
  that these methods can also be Before, After, etc.), and
  TestAnnotation (for specific information only available on the @Test
  annotation).
- Created TestMethodElement to encapsulate the relationship between
  @Test, @Before, and @After.  This class may go away again quickly
- Updated version in docs to 4.5
- Included docs about junit-dep jar

Run Different: Extending JUnit 4 with Custom Runners

<p>
Custom Runners are the primary means of extending JUnit 4.  A custom
runner can do anything from adding an extra check to the end of each
test method, to running tests written in a different test framework
(like TestNG) or a different language (like Ruby, or a completely new
domain-specific language).  Custom runners automatically get tight
integration with Eclipse, Ant, and other JUnit-4-aware development
tools. In addition, custom runners can make use of
JUnit's little-known test prioritization and filtering capabilities.
</p>

<p>
The talk will first demonstrate JUnit's built-in runners for
parameterized data and unit theories (general statements evaluated
against specific examples).  It will then review custom runners written by
third parties, including JMock's runner, the CPSuite runner for
automatically finding JUnit test classes, and the JRSpecRunner for
running RSpec tests written in Ruby.
</p>

<p>
Finally, audience members will learn the Custom Runner API as we write
together a new custom runner that runs many JUnit tests in parallel
threads.
</p>


Theory-infected: Or How I Learned To Stop Worrying And Use Unit Theories in JUnit 4

<p>
  Writing developer tests as software is built can provide peace of mind.  As
  the software grows, running the tests can prove that everything still works
  as the developer envisioned it.  But what about the behavior the developer
  failed to envision?  Although verifying a few well-picked scenarios is often
  enough, experienced developers know bugs can often lurk even in well-tested
  code, when correct but untested inputs provoke obviously wrong responses.
  This leads to worry.
</p>

<p>
  JUnit now allows writing unit <em>theories</em> alongside unit tests, to
  specify desired universal behaviors.  For example, when writing a
  date and time library, we can write a theory stating that adding an
  hour to any TimePoint produces a later time:

<pre>
	@Theory
	public void laterDateIsAfter(GlindaTimePoint a) {
		assertThat(a.plus(hours(1)), after(a));
	}
</pre>
</p>

<p>
  We will demonstrate how writing theories
  affects test-driven development, how new features in JUnit can verify
  theories against hand-picked inputs, and how a new tool, Theory Explorer,
  can search for new inputs, leading to a new, less worrysome approach to
  development.
</p>

#NOW 2007-11-30 10:20:20
Starting an hour-long spike on workflow now.

The presentation will use four different scenarios to demonstrate four
aspects of software development using Theories in JUnit: finding bugs
in an established code base, automatically generating collaborators,
test-driving development with theories, and verifying specifications
that are hard to express with design-by-contract.

\subsection{Finding bugs in an established code base}

We have found that many well-tested, widely-used libraries have test
suites that appear to be written with a specification in mind.
Individual scenarios are chosen to confirm corner cases of the general
specification, which itself does not appear explicitly in the test
suite, code, or documentation.  We will demonstrate one example of how
explicitly stating the general principle as a Theory not only makes
the intent clearer for future maintainers, but allows the discovery of
existing bugs using Theory Explorer.

Time and Money\footnote{\url{http://timeandmoney.domainlanguage.com/}}
is a library implementing time and currency domains in
a fluent interface based on Domain-Driven Design.\cite{DDD}.
One of its core classes is 
\code{Interval}, which defines a range of values between two endpoints, which
may be inclusive or exclusive.  We will show a a real test that asserts that the
complement of a particular Interval relative to itself is empty:

\begin{Verbatim}
public void testRelativeComplementEqual() {
  Interval c1_7c = Interval.closed(new Integer(1),
                                   new Integer(7));
  List complement = c1_7c.complementRelativeTo(c1_7c);
  assertEquals(0, complement.size());
}
\end{Verbatim}

This is true not only of closed intervals between 1 and 7,
but of any Interval.  We will show how writing this explicitly as a
Theory saves thought and typing:

\begin{Verbatim}
@Theory
public void relativeComplementOfSelfIsEmpty(Interval i) {
  List complement = i.complementRelativeTo(i);
  assertEquals(0, complement.size());
}
\end{Verbatim}

We will show how data points can be manually added to the test suite,
increasing test coverage and developer confidence.  Then, we will use
Theory Explorer to show an unexpected bug found in the wild (reported
in our companion research paper submission to ICSE (\emph{The Practice of
Theories: Adding ``For-all'' Statements to ``There-Exists'' Tests}).

We will argue that expressing specifications in Theories is a small
conceptual jump for developers already accustomed to testing in JUnit.

\subsection{Automatically generating collaborators}

A theory can specify exactly how a component A depends on another
component B, and let B vary in every other dimension.  For example,
consider an air-travel-planning algorithm which depends
PreferenceProvider interface, which may choose between flights based
on schedule, duration, or cost.  For example, this theory states that
if the PreferenceProvider will only consider non-stop flights, only
non-stop flights should be chosen:

\begin{Verbatim}
@Theory
public void nonstopPreference(FlightRequest r, PreferenceProvider p) {
  assumeThat(p.willAcceptLayover(), is(false));

  Plan plan = new Agent().plan(r);
  assumeThat(plan.successful(), is(true));

  assertThat(plan.layoverCount(), is(0));
}
\end{Verbatim}

The default implementation of the PreferenceProvider interface
consults a human through the user interface, but we would like to
avoid requiring a human to participate in every run of the unit tests.
We could construct by hand a stub PreferenceProvider that will provide
the assumed response, but this is repetitive work.  Instead, we will
show how the Diabol theory runner extension automatically generates a
stub for the PreferenceProvider class by iterative refinement.

\subsection{Test-driving development with Theories}

We will quickly show how a roman numeral conversion function can be
written test first, using one theory, a handful of explicit tests for
corner cases, and Theory Explorer to find remaining failing data
points.

\subsection{Theories compared to Design by Contract}

There are some ideas that are as easily expressed in the preconditions
and postconditions of a Design-by-Contract specification as they are
in a Theory.  However, Theories can often be much simpler.

For example, the following
Theory about stacks expresses an obvious relationship between two
mutating methods:

\begin{Verbatim}
@Theory public void pushThenPop(Stack s, Object o) {
  s.push(o);
  assertTrue(s.pop() == o);
}
\end{Verbatim}

Expressing this as preconditions and postconditions would require a
model of the Stack's abstract state, which can be more mental
investment than is possible or necessary.

#NOW 2007-12-13 00:33:35
OK, so it seems that the best option I have for choosing when to wake
the boys up is to split the difference between the sleep differential
from the previous day and the awake differential.  Yesterday, Isaac
slept from 10 to 6:30--8.5 hours sleep, and then was awake today
until, adjusting for the trip home, 10pm, so 15.5 hours awake.
Tomorrow, he'll go down for nap at 1pm.  If he wakes up at 6:30, the
sleep differential will be 0, and the awake differential will be 9
hours.  According to this idea, getting him up at 6:30 is ideal.

Ethan, on the other hand, slept 10 hours, and awake for 15.5.  The
zero point for him would be 8am--since he'll go to sleep at 8pm,
that's 12 hours awake.

#NOW 2007-12-15 15:10:20 Ethan used legos today to build a garden.
I'm not sure what all of the flowers in the garden were, but the red
block was roses.  He had used a blue block for water, but sadly the
blue block was part of Isaac's Lofty model, and had to be given back.
Ethan collapsed into tears--big, real tears--before I reminded him
that the smaller legos also could be attached to the duplo-sized
legos.

Just now, Ethan wanted to look over my shoulder as I was writing
this.  I told him no, and he launched into a snit, first trying to
move all of his toys into the hallways by Isaac's room, then elling me
that he had to "meet the director of his play", and that I was making
him angry because I was making him late.

This has escalated and escalated, with Ethan talking back to me each
time.  I just spanked him, yelled at him at the top of my lungs, and
put him on the bed in our room indefinitely.  Even then, he talked
back to me.  I currently feel several things.

- This cannot be the solution to the problem.  This doesn't scale to
  14.
- Maybe it does scale to 14.  Maybe when Ethan is 14, he will have bad
  days that have moments like this in the middle, just like we do
  now.  The problem is not that my approach is wrong, just that I
  don't have the backbone to put up with years of it.
- I hate spanking.  Spanking certainly does not scale to 14.  I go to
  spanking in hopes of sparking remorse--just once, I'd like Ethan to
  be sad because he'd made me upset, not just because he'd like to
  have a privilege that I've removed.
- I have to have a right to some time to myself in my own house.
  Ethan cannot get snitty with me just because I fail to jump when he
  says jump.
- I may ask for too much time to myself in my own house.  The kids
  don't necessarily see the difference between me talking to Grandma
  for an hour on the phone and playing video games for an hour--it's
  still time I'm not spending with them.
- The computer is a huge part of the problem.  It's what started all
  of this.  The computer can do anything--the fact that I am really
  trying to use it to organize my life appears to not mean anything--I
  might as well be playing Mario Brothers, because I'm Using The
  Computer--the ultimate Holy Grail.  Heck, Ethan will stare at MY
  DESKTOP IMAGE for minutes, just to absorb the wonder that is The
  Computer.
- Of course, that's part of the problem itself.  This is how I relax.
  I got a computer job and a computer research track because I enjoy
  working on computers.  Part of this is impudence, but part is the
  boys just truly sensing what I already value.  I don't feel like I
  spend too much time in front of the computer.  Perhaps I do?  What
  is the alternative?
- I know what the alternative is--I need to become a better
  homekeeper, have Grocery Tuesdays and Laundry Thursdays, and other
  routines and quick fixes that don't actually require a computer to
  get right.  I'm crappy at these things.
- I had nothing planned for today.  Actually, I had errands and a trip
  to the library planned, but then got caught up in getting ready for
  Christmas, and calling my parents, and wasted possibly the only
  errandable morning for a week.  If I'd been using Glinda, I don't
  think that would have happened, but I can't use Glinda, because that
  means using the computer, which I'm not allowed to do alone.
- I think that the Dr. Pepper is making me irritable.  I've got to
  stop.
- I can't blame Ethan, 4, with not coming up with enough productive
  things to do, when David, 31, is just as bad at coming up with
  them.
- I need to pray.  I really need to pray.  I have to get off my doughy
  white butt, and pray.

So what now?  In terms of ultimate causes, I have to start getting up
earlier than everyone else.  Ironically, I also need to ditch the
soda.  Proximately, Ethan can't talk back to me.  Also ultimately, the
kids need to see Becca and I work together better.  So:

1) Get a better alarm clock
2) Get rid of the soda
3) Work better with Becca
4) Talk to Ethan

Basement = 46300
Attic = 15800

46300 + 15800 = 62100
31050

Upstairs has 43.5 more square feet in bedroom + 18 square feet in the front room.

It appears space is valued at about $125/sqft, so this means the main
part of upstairs is worth $7687.50.  According to the assessment, the
downstairs porch was worth $5,200 more than the upstairs porch, but
that should probably be handled with money.  The basement is about 45
x 24 = 1080 sqft.  Nathan claims it's valued at 46300, meaning that
each square foot is worth $42.87.  Actually, we can subtract 12 x 10.5
= 126 sqft, which is the common entrance, worth $5401.67.  That leaves
954 square feet worth $40898.33.  The Swillings have an attic worth,
apparently, $15,800.  This means that currently, just basement to
attic, the Saffs are 25098 ahead.  If we give the Swillings 12,549.16,
we'd be square on basement/attic.  That's 292.73 sqft, which is 24.4
feet of one side.

#NOW 2008-01-04 09:25:12

So I'm going to have two more blogs.  What Doing is perfect.  Share
and Enjoy is too Douglas Adams: I want to be the top google hit on my
own blog name.  I'm probably spawning two blogs here--one for my own
occasional musings, and one for just tech stuff.  Ideas:

1) Testing the Sprites: a deliberate mistype of testing the spirits,
but my work doesn't particularly focus on sprites.

2) tech.david.saff.net: Doesn't add or subtract anything.

3) Language of Demands: This gets into Popper territory.  I think I
have things to offer to programming in this respect, but Google shows
that "Language of Demands" has a negative impact

4) Fail fast: pretty hard to argue with, but maybe not pointing to me
as a person.

5) junit.saff.net: A little too specific, even though most of my work
is Java-based.  I'd like this blog to survive a leap to Ruby, if not
necessarily a jump to artificial intelligence

6) Wooba wooba: I don't like the grover connotation

7) Test Driving: Goes out on a limb, apparently, while still giving
the impression of "test driving" a car.  The second definition is the
only one Google comes up with.

8) Name the page after my eventual company.  This has a big
advantage.  Perhaps shouldbewillbe.com?  This was to be my world-wide
bugzilla page.

9) TOTE, or test operate test exit.  Hard to get high on Google.

10) Some punny variation on test operate test exit
